[2024-10-01 12:00] Sarah Roberts: That's a fascinating distinction. Are you referring to their semantic boundaries in Classical Arabic, or more about their conceptual mapping in the Quranic ontology?
[2024-10-01 12:01] Paul Turner: Both, actually. In the corpus, 'dust' and 'clay' seem to be used interchangeably in some verses, but the semantic weight is different. The translators handled it inconsistently.
[2024-10-01 12:01] Sarah Roberts: I see. Do you have specific verses in mind? Sometimes context can help clarify the intended meaning.
[2024-10-01 12:02] Paul Turner: Yes, for example, Surah Al-Mu’minun:12-14. The sequence goes from 'dust' to 'clay' to 'sperm-drop'. The gradation is important, but the Arabic terms—'turab' and 'teen'—don’t always map neatly onto 'dust' and 'clay' in English.
[2024-10-01 12:03] Sarah Roberts: That gradation does seem deliberate. 'Turab' is definitely 'dust' or 'soil', but 'teen' is closer to 'clay'—moist, moldable earth. Have you looked at how these are glossed in early tafsir?
[2024-10-01 12:03] Paul Turner: I have. Al-Tabari and Ibn Kathir both distinguish them, but modern translators sometimes collapse the terms for readability. It muddies the ontology for digital mapping.
[2024-10-01 12:04] Sarah Roberts: That’s a challenge. In sign languages, especially Levantine dialects, the signs for 'dust' and 'clay' are distinct, likely reflecting the same conceptual separation. Have you considered cross-referencing with semantic typology in regional sign languages?
[2024-10-01 12:05] Paul Turner: I haven’t. That’s an intriguing idea. If the distinction is preserved in sign, it might reinforce the need to keep them separate in the ontology.
[2024-10-01 12:05] Sarah Roberts: Exactly. And from a typological perspective, the way languages encode material states—dry, moist, inert, fertile—can be quite revealing. It’s almost a cognitive mapping.
[2024-10-01 12:06] Paul Turner: That’s precisely what I’m struggling with. In the digital taxonomy, each concept needs a discrete node. I’m worried about overgeneralization.
[2024-10-01 12:06] Sarah Roberts: Would it help to create a hierarchy? For example, 'earth' as the superordinate, with 'dust' and 'clay' as subordinate categories, each with their own attributes.
[2024-10-01 12:07] Paul Turner: Yes, that’s what I was leaning towards. But then some verses refer to 'dust' metaphorically—impermanence, mortality—while 'clay' seems to relate to creation and formation.
[2024-10-01 12:07] Sarah Roberts: That’s where pragmatic analysis comes in. Maybe annotate each node with contextual usage—literal, metaphorical, ritual, etc.
[2024-10-01 12:08] Paul Turner: I like that. It would allow for more nuanced queries. But it’s a lot of manual annotation.
[2024-10-01 12:08] Sarah Roberts: True. But you could automate the initial mapping using frequency analysis, then manually refine edge cases. Have you tried corpus linguistics tools for this?
[2024-10-01 12:09] Paul Turner: I’ve used basic keyword searches, but nothing sophisticated. Most tools struggle with non-standard semantic fields in religious texts.
[2024-10-01 12:09] Sarah Roberts: I can help set up a feature extraction model. Maybe use Python for initial clustering, then check the clusters against established tafsir.
[2024-10-01 12:10] Paul Turner: That would be brilliant. Do you think syntactic dependencies would help, or should we rely more on collocation statistics?
[2024-10-01 12:10] Sarah Roberts: Collocation first, then supplement with dependency parsing if the clusters are fuzzy. Arabic syntax can be tricky, especially in older manuscripts.
[2024-10-01 12:11] Paul Turner: Agreed. By the way, have you come across any sign language grammars that treat material states in a way that could inform this?
[2024-10-01 12:11] Sarah Roberts: Yes, actually. Some Levantine dialects have classifiers for states—solid, powder, liquid. 'Dust' is signed with a fluttering motion, 'clay' with a molding gesture. They’re never conflated.
[2024-10-01 12:12] Paul Turner: That’s a compelling parallel. It could strengthen my argument for keeping the categories distinct in the ontology.
[2024-10-01 12:12] Sarah Roberts: It would. And if you want, I can share some field notes from Beirut—local signers are very precise with earth materials, especially in religious contexts.
[2024-10-01 12:13] Paul Turner: Please do. I’d love to see how the conceptual mapping works in real dialogue. It might even convince my more skeptical colleagues.

[2024-11-12 08:00] Sarah Roberts: I submitted my paper on sign language phonology to the Journal of Linguistics yesterday.
[2024-11-12 08:01] Paul Turner: Congratulations, Sarah! That’s a significant milestone. How do you feel about it?
[2024-11-12 08:02] Sarah Roberts: Relieved, mostly. It took longer than I expected—especially the section on phonological variation in Levantine Sign Languages.
[2024-11-12 08:03] Paul Turner: I can imagine. Did you cover the typological aspects as well, or was it more focused on the phonology itself?
[2024-11-12 08:04] Sarah Roberts: Both, actually. I argued that the phonological systems are shaped by contact with local spoken Arabic varieties, which complicates classification.
[2024-11-12 08:05] Paul Turner: That’s fascinating. Did you include examples of relexification, or was that outside the scope?
[2024-11-12 08:06] Sarah Roberts: I touched on it briefly. There’s a clear pattern of lexical borrowing, especially in urban settings, but the journal editors wanted me to keep the focus on phonology.
[2024-11-12 08:07] Paul Turner: Makes sense. Did you encounter any resistance when framing sign language phonology in relation to spoken language systems?
[2024-11-12 08:08] Sarah Roberts: Yes, definitely. There’s still a strong bias privileging spoken over signed modalities. I tried to address that in the introduction.
[2024-11-12 08:09] Paul Turner: It reminds me of the challenges I face with Quranic ontology. Traditional scholars are often skeptical of unconventional frameworks.
[2024-11-12 08:10] Sarah Roberts: Absolutely. I remember your talk on digital taxonomy for Quranic concepts. Did you ever resolve the mapping issues with Classical Arabic nuances?
[2024-11-12 08:11] Paul Turner: Not entirely. Some concepts are so context-dependent that rigid ontologies become almost counterproductive.
[2024-11-12 08:12] Sarah Roberts: That’s so true. In my case, transcribing sign parameters for comparative analysis is tricky—especially when there’s no standardized notation for certain movements.
[2024-11-12 08:13] Paul Turner: Are you using Stokoe notation or something more recent?
[2024-11-12 08:14] Sarah Roberts: I started with Stokoe, but ended up adapting it with elements of HamNoSys. It’s not perfect, but it allows for more granularity.
[2024-11-12 08:15] Paul Turner: HamNoSys is quite elegant. I wish we had something as flexible for Quranic semantics—mapping from root letters to conceptual nodes is a labyrinth.

[2024-11-20 08:00] Paul Turner: I'm thinking of writing a comparative piece on Quranic and Biblical cosmology.
[2024-11-20 08:01] Sarah Roberts: That’s fascinating, Paul. Are you planning to focus on the ontological aspects or more on the narrative structures of creation?
[2024-11-20 08:02] Paul Turner: Primarily the ontology, actually. I want to examine how the concept of "Earth" is classified and described in both scriptures. There’s so much nuance in the Quranic corpus, especially compared to some of the Biblical accounts.
[2024-11-20 08:03] Sarah Roberts: Absolutely. The variation in terminology alone is a rich field. In sign language studies, I’ve noticed that cosmological concepts often get relexified quite differently from spoken traditions.
[2024-11-20 08:04] Paul Turner: That’s intriguing. How do Levantine sign languages, for example, express “Earth” or “creation”? Do they borrow from spoken Arabic, or are there unique signs?
[2024-11-20 08:05] Sarah Roberts: There’s a blend. Some dialects use a gesture resembling the physical globe, while others borrow morphemes from Arabic. But the semantic boundaries can shift, especially in communities exposed to multiple languages and religions.
[2024-11-20 08:06] Paul Turner: That aligns with something I've seen in the Quranic text—where “al-ard” is sometimes the physical planet, sometimes land, and occasionally a spiritual domain. Mapping that into a digital ontology is tricky.
[2024-11-20 08:07] Sarah Roberts: Do you find the traditional Tafsir helps, or does it complicate things more when you're trying to digitize those meanings?
[2024-11-20 08:08] Paul Turner: Both, honestly. Tafsir gives rich context, but its interpretive flexibility makes formal classification hard. The same word can have multiple readings, which digital systems struggle with.
[2024-11-20 08:09] Sarah Roberts: I encounter similar issues with sign language documentation. The visual modality allows for polysemy that resists strict categorization. Sometimes, meaning depends heavily on context or even the signer’s intent.
[2024-11-20 08:10] Paul Turner: That’s an interesting parallel. If I may ask, does the sign language corpus you’re working on align signs for “earth” with spiritual or mythic concepts, or is it mostly secular?
[2024-11-20 08:11] Sarah Roberts: There’s definitely overlap. In some communities, the sign for “earth” doubles as “life” or “world” in religious contexts. I’ve seen interpreters modify gestures for sermons or religious events, adding layers of meaning.
[2024-11-20 08:12] Paul Turner: That’s almost poetic. It reminds me of the metaphorical uses in scripture—like the “new earth” or “promised land” in both traditions. There’s a constant tension between literal and figurative.

[2024-12-12 09:00] Paul Turner: I've written the first chapter of the cosmology book. It's flowing well.

[2024-12-12 09:02] Paul Turner: Yes, exactly. I’ve managed to frame the opening around the Quranic concept of Earth, tying in both ontology and comparative cosmology.
[2024-12-12 09:03] Kathleen Green: Intriguing. How are you approaching the symbolism? Are you leaning more toward literal interpretations or looking at metaphorical layers?
[2024-12-12 09:04] Paul Turner: I’m balancing both. The literal verses anchor the semantic mapping, but the metaphors are what allow for philosophical depth.
[2024-12-12 09:05] Kathleen Green: That reminds me of Yeats and his use of esoteric symbolism—how the spiritual and physical spheres interlace. Do you find similar patterns in the Quran?
[2024-12-12 09:06] Paul Turner: Absolutely. There’s a recurring theme of Earth as both a created entity and a spiritual stage. The duality is quite profound.
[2024-12-12 09:07] Kathleen Green: It sounds like you’re charting new territory. Are you referencing traditional tafsir or focusing on your own computational taxonomy?
[2024-12-12 09:08] Paul Turner: Both. I begin with classical exegesis, then re-contextualize it through digital ontology. The challenge is bridging the gap without losing nuance.
[2024-12-12 09:09] Kathleen Green: I imagine some scholars might resist the digital approach. Have you had any pushback so far?
[2024-12-12 09:10] Paul Turner: A bit, yes. There’s skepticism, especially regarding algorithmic mapping of meanings. But the first chapter aims to show the possibilities.
[2024-12-12 09:11] Kathleen Green: I’m curious, do you relate these cosmological ideas to artistic expressions? Mondrian, for instance, saw geometry as spiritual.
[2024-12-12 09:12] Paul Turner: That’s a fascinating parallel. I mention the intersection, especially how abstraction in art mirrors abstraction in theological texts.
[2024-12-12 09:13] Kathleen Green: Wonderful. Have you found any Quranic passages that resonate with modernist or abstract aesthetics?
[2024-12-12 09:14] Paul Turner: Yes—certain verses about creation and order echo the minimalist structure found in modernist art. It’s a subtle but powerful connection.
[2024-12-12 09:15] Kathleen Green: I’d love to read that section. Does your writing style lean toward analytic or do you allow for poetic interpretation?
[2024-12-12 09:16] Paul Turner: I start analytically, but inevitably some poetry slips in. The subject almost demands it.
